   <h1 class="firstHeading" id="firstHeading" lang="en">
    Invasive species
   </h1>
   <div class="mw-body-content" id="bodyContent">
    <div id="siteSub">
     From Wikipedia, the free encyclopedia
    </div>
    <div id="contentSub">
    </div>
    <div class="mw-jump" id="jump-to-nav">
     Jump to:
     <a href="#mw-head">
      navigation
     </a>
     ,
     <a href="#p-search">
      search
     </a>
    </div>
    <div class="mw-content-ltr" dir="ltr" id="mw-content-text" lang="en">
     <div class="hatnote" role="note">
      See also:
      <a href="/wiki/Introduced_species" title="Introduced species">
       Introduced species
      </a>
      ,
      <a href="/wiki/Pest_(organism)" title="Pest (organism)">
       Pest (organism)
      </a>
      , and
      <a href="/wiki/Weed" title="Weed">
       Weed
      </a>
     </div>
     <div class="thumb tright">
      <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
       <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Beaver_dam_in_Tierra_del_Fuego.jpg">
        <img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1536" data-file-width="2048" height="165" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c2/Beaver_dam_in_Tierra_del_Fuego.jpg/220px-Beaver_dam_in_Tierra_del_Fuego.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c2/Beaver_dam_in_Tierra_del_Fuego.jpg/330px-Beaver_dam_in_Tierra_del_Fuego.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c2/Beaver_dam_in_Tierra_del_Fuego.jpg/440px-Beaver_dam_in_Tierra_del_Fuego.jpg 2x" width="220"/>
       </a>
       <div class="thumbcaption">
        <div class="magnify">
         <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Beaver_dam_in_Tierra_del_Fuego.jpg" title="Enlarge">
         </a>
        </div>
        <a href="/wiki/Beaver_eradication_in_Tierra_del_Fuego" title="Beaver eradication in Tierra del Fuego">
         Beavers from North America constitute an invasive species
        </a>
        in
        <a href="/wiki/Tierra_del_Fuego" title="Tierra del Fuego">
         Tierra del Fuego
        </a>
        , where they have a substantial impact on landscape and local ecology through
        <a href="/wiki/Beaver_dam" title="Beaver dam">
         their dams
        </a>
        .
       </div>
      </div>
     </div>
     <div class="thumb tright">
      <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
       <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Kudzu_on_trees_in_Atlanta,_Georgia.jpg">
        <img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1242" data-file-width="2016" height="136" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4c/Kudzu_on_trees_in_Atlanta%2C_Georgia.jpg/220px-Kudzu_on_trees_in_Atlanta%2C_Georgia.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4c/Kudzu_on_trees_in_Atlanta%2C_Georgia.jpg/330px-Kudzu_on_trees_in_Atlanta%2C_Georgia.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4c/Kudzu_on_trees_in_Atlanta%2C_Georgia.jpg/440px-Kudzu_on_trees_in_Atlanta%2C_Georgia.jpg 2x" width="220"/>
       </a>
       <div class="thumbcaption">
        <div class="magnify">
         <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Kudzu_on_trees_in_Atlanta,_Georgia.jpg" title="Enlarge">
         </a>
        </div>
        <a href="/wiki/Kudzu" title="Kudzu">
         Kudzu
        </a>
        , a Japanese vine species invasive in the southeast United States, growing in
        <a href="/wiki/Atlanta" title="Atlanta">
         Atlanta
        </a>
        ,
        <a href="/wiki/Georgia_(U.S._state)" title="Georgia (U.S. state)">
         Georgia
        </a>
       </div>
      </div>
     </div>
     <p>
      An
      <b>
       invasive species
      </b>
      is a
      <a href="/wiki/Plant" title="Plant">
       plant
      </a>
      ,
      <a href="/wiki/Fungus" title="Fungus">
       fungus
      </a>
      , or
      <a href="/wiki/Animal" title="Animal">
       animal
      </a>
      <a href="/wiki/Species" title="Species">
       species
      </a>
      that is not native to a specific location (an
      <a href="/wiki/Introduced_species" title="Introduced species">
       introduced species
      </a>
      ), and which has a tendency to spread to a degree believed to cause damage to the environment, human economy or human health.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-1">
       <a href="#cite_note-1">
        [1]
       </a>
      </sup>
      <sup class="noprint Inline-Template" style="white-space:nowrap;">
       [
       <i>
        <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Disputed_statement" title="Wikipedia:Disputed statement">
         <span title="The material near this tag is possibly inaccurate or nonfactual. (October 2015)">
          dubious
         </span>
        </a>
        <span class="metadata">
         –
         <a href="/wiki/Talk:Invasive_species#Dubious" title="Talk:Invasive species">
          discuss
         </a>
        </span>
       </i>
       ]
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      One study pointed out widely divergent perceptions of the criteria for invasive species among researchers (p. 135) and concerns with the subjectivity of the term "invasive" (p. 136).
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-neutral_2-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-neutral-2">
        [2]
       </a>
      </sup>
      Some of the alternate usages of the term are below:
     </p>
     <ul>
      <li>
       The term as most often used applies to introduced species (also called "non-indigenous" or "non-native") that adversely affect the
       <a href="/wiki/Habitat" title="Habitat">
        habitats
       </a>
       and
       <a href="/wiki/Bioregion" title="Bioregion">
        bioregions
       </a>
       they invade economically, environmentally, or ecologically. Such invasive species may be either plants or animals and may disrupt by dominating a region,
       <a href="/wiki/Wilderness" title="Wilderness">
        wilderness areas
       </a>
       , particular
       <a href="/wiki/Habitat" title="Habitat">
        habitats
       </a>
       , or
       <a href="/wiki/Wildland%E2%80%93urban_interface" title="Wildland–urban interface">
        wildland–urban interface
       </a>
       land from loss of natural controls (such as
       <a href="/wiki/Predation" title="Predation">
        predators
       </a>
       or
       <a href="/wiki/Herbivore" title="Herbivore">
        herbivores
       </a>
       ). This includes non-native
       <a href="/wiki/Category:Invasive_plant_species" title="Category:Invasive plant species">
        invasive plant species
       </a>
       labeled as
       <b>
        exotic pest plants
       </b>
       and
       <b>
        invasive exotics
       </b>
       growing in native
       <a href="/wiki/Plant_community" title="Plant community">
        plant communities
       </a>
       .
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-3">
        <a href="#cite_note-3">
         [3]
        </a>
       </sup>
       It has been used in this sense by
       <a href="/wiki/Government" title="Government">
        government
       </a>
       organizations
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-4">
        <a href="#cite_note-4">
         [4]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-5">
        <a href="#cite_note-5">
         [5]
        </a>
       </sup>
       as well as conservation groups such as the
       <a href="/wiki/International_Union_for_Conservation_of_Nature" title="International Union for Conservation of Nature">
        International Union for Conservation of Nature
       </a>
       (IUCN) and the
       <a href="/wiki/California_Native_Plant_Society" title="California Native Plant Society">
        California Native Plant Society
       </a>
       .
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-neutral_2-1">
        <a href="#cite_note-neutral-2">
         [2]
        </a>
       </sup>
       The
       <a href="/wiki/European_Union" title="European Union">
        European Union
       </a>
       defines "Invasive Alien Species" as those that are, firstly, outside their natural distribution area, and secondly, threaten
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Biological_diversity" title="Biological diversity">
        biological diversity
       </a>
       .
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-6">
        <a href="#cite_note-6">
         [6]
        </a>
       </sup>
       It is also used by land managers, botanists, researchers, horticulturalists, conservationists, and the public for
       <a href="/wiki/Noxious_weed" title="Noxious weed">
        noxious weeds
       </a>
       .
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-7">
        <a href="#cite_note-7">
         [7]
        </a>
       </sup>
       The
       <a href="/wiki/Kudzu" title="Kudzu">
        kudzu vine
       </a>
       (
       <i>
        Pueraria lobata
       </i>
       ),
       <a href="/wiki/Cortaderia_jubata" title="Cortaderia jubata">
        Andean Pampas grass
       </a>
       (
       <i>
        Cortaderia jubata
       </i>
       ), and
       <a href="/wiki/Centaurea_solstitialis" title="Centaurea solstitialis">
        yellow starthistle
       </a>
       (
       <i>
        Centaurea solstitialis
       </i>
       ) are examples.
      </li>
      <li>
       An alternate usage broadens the term to include indigenous or "native" species along with
       <i>
        non-native
       </i>
       species, that have colonized natural areas (p. 136).
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-neutral_2-2">
        <a href="#cite_note-neutral-2">
         [2]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <a href="/wiki/Deer" title="Deer">
        Deer
       </a>
       are an example, considered to be overpopulating their native zones and adjacent suburban gardens, by some in the
       <a href="/wiki/Northeastern_United_States" title="Northeastern United States">
        Northeastern
       </a>
       and
       <a href="/wiki/West_Coast_of_the_United_States" title="West Coast of the United States">
        Pacific Coast
       </a>
       regions of the United States.
       <sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">
        [
        <i>
         <a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed">
          <span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (August 2014)">
           citation needed
          </span>
         </a>
        </i>
        ]
       </sup>
      </li>
      <li>
       Sometimes the term is used to describe a non-native or
       <a href="/wiki/Introduced_species" title="Introduced species">
        introduced species
       </a>
       that has become widespread (p. 136).
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-neutral_2-3">
        <a href="#cite_note-neutral-2">
         [2]
        </a>
       </sup>
       However, not every
       <a href="/wiki/Introduced_species" title="Introduced species">
        introduced species
       </a>
       has adverse effects on the environment. A nonadverse example is the common goldfish (
       <i>
        <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Carassius_auratus" title="Carassius auratus">
         Carassius auratus
        </a>
       </i>
       ), which is found throughout the United States, but rarely achieves high densities (p. 136).
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-neutral_2-4">
        <a href="#cite_note-neutral-2">
         [2]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </li>
     </ul>

           <a href="#Health">
            <span class="tocnumber">
             3.4
            </span>
            <span class="toctext">
             Health
            </span>
           </a>
          </li>
          <li class="toclevel-2 tocsection-22">
           <a href="#Biodiversity">
            <span class="tocnumber">
             3.5
            </span>
            <span class="toctext">
             Biodiversity
            </span>
           </a>
           <ul>
            <li class="toclevel-3 tocsection-23">
             <a href="#Genetic_pollution">
              <span class="tocnumber">
               3.5.1
              </span>
              <span class="toctext">
               Genetic pollution
              </span>
             </a>
            </li>
           </ul>
          </li>
         </ul>
        </li>
        <li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-24">
         <a href="#Study">
          <span class="tocnumber">
           4
          </span>
          <span class="toctext">
           Study
          </span>
         </a>
        </li>
        <li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-25">
         <a href="#See_also">
          <span class="tocnumber">
           5
          </span>
          <span class="toctext">
           See also
          </span>
         </a>
        </li>
        <li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-26">
         <a href="#References">
          <span class="tocnumber">
           6
          </span>
          <span class="toctext">
           References
          </span>
         </a>
        </li>
        <li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-27">
         <a href="#External_links">
          <span class="tocnumber">
           7
          </span>
          <span class="toctext">
           External links
          </span>
         </a>
        </li>
       </ul>
      </div>
     </div>
     <h2>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="Causes">
       Causes
      </span>
      <span class="mw-editsection">
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        [
       </span>
       <a href="/w/index.php?title=Invasive_species&amp;action=edit&amp;section=1" title="Edit section: Causes">
        edit
       </a>
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        ]
       </span>
      </span>
     </h2>
     <p>
      Scientists include species- and ecosystem factors among the mechanisms that when combined, establish invasiveness in a newly introduced species.
     </p>
     <h3>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="Species-based_mechanisms">
       Species-based mechanisms
      </span>
      <span class="mw-editsection">
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        [
       </span>
       <a href="/w/index.php?title=Invasive_species&amp;action=edit&amp;section=2" title="Edit section: Species-based mechanisms">
        edit
       </a>
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        ]
       </span>
      </span>
     </h3>
     <p>
      While all species compete to survive, invasive species appear to have specific traits or specific combinations of traits that allow them to outcompete
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Native_species" title="Native species">
       native species
      </a>
      . In some cases, the competition is about rates of growth and reproduction. In other cases, species interact with each other more directly.
     </p>
     <p>
      Researchers disagree about the usefulness of traits as invasiveness markers. One study found that of a list of invasive and noninvasive species, 86% of the invasive species could be identified from the traits alone.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-kolar_8-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-kolar-8">
        [8]
       </a>
      </sup>
      Another study found invasive species tended to have only a small subset of the presumed traits and that many similar traits were found in noninvasive species, requiring other explanations.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-kolar_8-1">
       <a href="#cite_note-kolar-8">
        [8]
       </a>
      </sup>
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-theb_9-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-theb-9">
        [9]
       </a>
      </sup>
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-reichard_10-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-reichard-10">
        [10]
       </a>
      </sup>
      Common invasive species traits include the following:
     </p>
     <ul>
      <li>
       Fast growth
      </li>
      <li>
       Rapid
       <a href="/wiki/Reproduction" title="Reproduction">
        reproduction
       </a>
      </li>
      <li>
       High
       <a href="/wiki/Biological_dispersal" title="Biological dispersal">
        dispersal
       </a>
       ability
      </li>
      <li>
       <a href="/wiki/Phenotypic_plasticity" title="Phenotypic plasticity">
        Phenotypic plasticity
       </a>
       (the ability to alter growth form to suit current conditions)
      </li>
      <li>
       Tolerance of a wide range of environmental conditions (
       <a href="/wiki/Ecological_competence" title="Ecological competence">
        Ecological competence
       </a>
       )
      </li>
      <li>
       Ability to live off of a wide range of food types (
       <a href="/wiki/Generalist_and_specialist_species" title="Generalist and specialist species">
        generalist
       </a>
       )
      </li>
      <li>
       Association with humans
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-williams_11-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-williams-11">
         [11]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </li>
      <li>
       Prior successful invasions
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ewell_12-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-ewell-12">
         [12]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </li>
     </ul>
     <p>
      Typically, an introduced species must survive at low population densities before it becomes invasive in a new location.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-tilm_13-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-tilm-13">
        [13]
       </a>
      </sup>
      At low population densities, it can be difficult for the introduced species to reproduce and maintain itself in a new location, so a species might reach a location multiple times before it becomes established. Repeated patterns of human movement, such as ships sailing to and from ports or cars driving up and down highways offer repeated opportunities for establishment (also known as a high
      <a href="/wiki/Propagule_pressure" title="Propagule pressure">
       propagule pressure
      </a>
      ).
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-verl_14-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-verl-14">
        [14]
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      An introduced species might become invasive if it can outcompete native species for resources such as
      <a href="/wiki/Nutrient" title="Nutrient">
       nutrients
      </a>
      , light, physical space, water, or food. If these species evolved under great
      <a href="/wiki/Competition_(biology)" title="Competition (biology)">
       competition
      </a>
      or
      <a href="/wiki/Predation" title="Predation">
       predation
      </a>
      , then the new environment may host fewer able competitors, allowing the invader to proliferate quickly. Ecosystems in which are being used to their fullest capacity by native species can be modeled as
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Zero-sum" title="Zero-sum">
       zero-sum
      </a>
      systems in which any gain for the invader is a loss for the native. However, such
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Unilateral" title="Unilateral">
       unilateral
      </a>
      competitive superiority (and extinction of native species with increased populations of the invader) is not the rule.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-stoh_15-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-stoh-15">
        [15]
       </a>
      </sup>
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-sax_16-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-sax-16">
        [16]
       </a>
      </sup>
      Invasive species often coexist with native species for an extended time, and gradually, the superior competitive ability of an invasive species becomes apparent as its population grows larger and denser and it adapts to its new location.
     </p>
     <div class="thumb tright">
      <div class="thumbinner" style="width:402px;">
       <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Lantana_Invasion_of_abandoned_citrus_plantation_Sdey_Hemed_Israel.JPG">
        <img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="726" data-file-width="2560" height="113" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/16/Lantana_Invasion_of_abandoned_citrus_plantation_Sdey_Hemed_Israel.JPG/400px-Lantana_Invasion_of_abandoned_citrus_plantation_Sdey_Hemed_Israel.JPG" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/16/Lantana_Invasion_of_abandoned_citrus_plantation_Sdey_Hemed_Israel.JPG/600px-Lantana_Invasion_of_abandoned_citrus_plantation_Sdey_Hemed_Israel.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/16/Lantana_Invasion_of_abandoned_citrus_plantation_Sdey_Hemed_Israel.JPG/800px-Lantana_Invasion_of_abandoned_citrus_plantation_Sdey_Hemed_Israel.JPG 2x" width="400"/>
       </a>
       <div class="thumbcaption">
        <div class="magnify">
         <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Lantana_Invasion_of_abandoned_citrus_plantation_Sdey_Hemed_Israel.JPG" title="Enlarge">
         </a>
        </div>
        <a href="/wiki/Lantana" title="Lantana">
         Lantana
        </a>
        growing in abandoned
        <a href="/wiki/Citrus" title="Citrus">
         citrus
        </a>
        <a href="/wiki/Plantation" title="Plantation">
         plantation
        </a>
        ;
        <a href="/wiki/Moshav" title="Moshav">
         Moshav
        </a>
        <a href="/wiki/Sdei_Hemed" title="Sdei Hemed">
         Sdei Hemed
        </a>
        ,
        <a href="/wiki/Israel" title="Israel">
         Israel
        </a>
       </div>
      </div>
     </div>
     <p>
      An invasive species might be able to use resources that were previously unavailable to native species, such as deep water sources accessed by a long
      <a href="/wiki/Taproot" title="Taproot">
       taproot
      </a>
      , or an ability to live on previously uninhabited soil types. For example, barbed goatgrass (
      <i>
       <a href="/wiki/Aegilops_triuncialis" title="Aegilops triuncialis">
        Aegilops triuncialis
       </a>
      </i>
      ) was introduced to
      <a href="/wiki/California" title="California">
       California
      </a>
      on
      <a href="/wiki/Serpentine_soil" title="Serpentine soil">
       serpentine soils
      </a>
      , which have low water-retention, low nutrient levels, a high
      <a href="/wiki/Magnesium" title="Magnesium">
       magnesium
      </a>
      /
      <a href="/wiki/Calcium" title="Calcium">
       calcium
      </a>
      ratio, and possible
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Heavy_metal_(chemistry)" title="Heavy metal (chemistry)">
       heavy metal
      </a>
      toxicity. Plant populations on these soils tend to show low density, but goatgrass can form dense stands on these soils and crowd out native species that have adapted poorly to serpentine soils.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-17">
       <a href="#cite_note-17">
        [17]
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      Invasive species might alters its environment by releasing chemical compounds, modifying
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Abiotic" title="Abiotic">
       abiotic
      </a>
      factors, or affecting the behaviour of herbivores, creating a positive or negative impact on other species. Some species, like
      <i>
       <a href="/wiki/Bryophyllum_daigremontianum" title="Bryophyllum daigremontianum">
        Kalanchoe daigremontana
       </a>
      </i>
      , produce
      <a href="/wiki/Allelopathy" title="Allelopathy">
       allelopathic compounds
      </a>
      , that might have an inhibitory effect on competing species. Other species like
      <i>
       <a href="/wiki/Stapelia_gigantea" title="Stapelia gigantea">
        Stapelia gigantea
       </a>
      </i>
      <a href="/wiki/Ecological_facilitation" title="Ecological facilitation">
       facilitates
      </a>
      the recruitment of seedlings of other species in arid environments by providing appropriate microclimatic conditions and preventing herbivory in early stages of development.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-18">
       <a href="#cite_note-18">
        [18]
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      Another examples are
      <i>
       <a href="/wiki/Centaurea_solstitialis" title="Centaurea solstitialis">
        Centaurea solstitialis
       </a>
      </i>
      (yellow starthistle) and
      <i>
       Centaurea diffusa
      </i>
      (
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Diffuse_knapweed" title="Diffuse knapweed">
       diffuse knapweed
      </a>
      ). These
      <a href="/wiki/Eastern_Europe" title="Eastern Europe">
       Eastern European
      </a>
      <a href="/wiki/Noxious_weed" title="Noxious weed">
       noxious weeds
      </a>
      have spread through the
      <a href="/wiki/Western_United_States" title="Western United States">
       western
      </a>
      and
      <a href="/wiki/West_Coast_of_the_United_States" title="West Coast of the United States">
       West Coast states
      </a>
      . Experiments show that
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/8-hydroxyquinoline" title="8-hydroxyquinoline">
       8-hydroxyquinoline
      </a>
      , a chemical produced at the root of
      <i>
       C. diffusa
      </i>
      , has a negative effect only on plants that have not co-evolved with it. Such co-evolved native plants have also evolved defenses.
      <i>
       C. diffusa
      </i>
      and
      <i>
       C. solstitialis
      </i>
      do not appear in their native habitats to be overwhelmingly successful competitors. Success or lack of success in one habitat does not necessarily imply success in others. Conversely, examining habitats in which a species is less successful can reveal novel weapons to defeat invasiveness.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-19">
       <a href="#cite_note-19">
        [19]
       </a>
      </sup>
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-vivan_20-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-vivan-20">
        [20]
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      Changes in fire regimens are another form of facilitation.
      <i>
       <a href="/wiki/Bromus_tectorum" title="Bromus tectorum">
        Bromus tectorum
       </a>
      </i>
      , originally from Eurasia, is highly fire-adapted. It not only spreads rapidly after burning but also increases the frequency and intensity (heat) of fires by providing large amounts of dry
      <a href="/wiki/Detritus" title="Detritus">
       detritus
      </a>
      during the fire season in western North America. In areas where it is widespread, it has altered the local fire regimen so much that native plants cannot survive the frequent fires, allowing
      <i>
       B. tectorum
      </i>
      to further extend and maintain dominance in its introduced range.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Brooks_2004_677.E2.80.93688_21-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-Brooks_2004_677.E2.80.93688-21">
        [21]
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      Facilitation also occurs where one species physically modifies a habitat in ways that are advantageous to other species. For example,
      <a href="/wiki/Zebra_mussel" title="Zebra mussel">
       zebra mussels
      </a>
      increase habitat complexity on lake floors, providing crevices in which
      <a href="/wiki/Invertebrate" title="Invertebrate">
       invertebrates
      </a>
      live. This increase in complexity, together with the nutrition provided by the waste products of mussel
      <a href="/wiki/Filter_feeder" title="Filter feeder">
       filter-feeding
      </a>
      , increases the density and diversity of
      <a href="/wiki/Benthic_zone" title="Benthic zone">
       benthic
      </a>
      invertebrate communities.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-silv_22-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-silv-22">
        [22]
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <h3>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="Ecosystem-based_mechanisms">
       Ecosystem-based mechanisms
      </span>
      <span class="mw-editsection">
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        [
       </span>
       <a href="/w/index.php?title=Invasive_species&amp;action=edit&amp;section=3" title="Edit section: Ecosystem-based mechanisms">
        edit
       </a>
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        ]
       </span>
      </span>
     </h3>
     <p>
      In
      <a href="/wiki/Ecosystem" title="Ecosystem">
       ecosystems
      </a>
      , the amount of available resources and the extent to which those resources are used by organisms determines the effects of additional species on the ecosystem. In stable ecosystems, equilibrium exists in the use of available resources. These mechanisms describe a situation in which the ecosystem has suffered a disturbance, which changes the fundamental nature of the ecosystem.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-23">
       <a href="#cite_note-23">
        [23]
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      When changes such as a
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Forest_fire" title="Forest fire">
       forest fire
      </a>
      occur, normal succession favors native
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Grass" title="Grass">
       grasses
      </a>
      and
      <a href="/wiki/Forb" title="Forb">
       forbs
      </a>
      . An introduced species that can spread faster than natives can use resources that would have been available to native species, squeezing them out.
      <a href="/wiki/Nitrogen" title="Nitrogen">
       Nitrogen
      </a>
      and
      <a href="/wiki/Phosphorus" title="Phosphorus">
       phosphorus
      </a>
      are often the limiting factors in these situations.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Davis_2000_528.E2.80.93534_24-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-Davis_2000_528.E2.80.93534-24">
        [24]
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      Every species occupies a
      <i>
       <a href="/wiki/Ecological_niche" title="Ecological niche">
        niche
       </a>
      </i>
      in its native ecosystem; some species fill large and varied roles, while others are highly specialized. Some invading species fill niches that are not used by native species, and they also can create new niches.
      <sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">
       [
       <i>
        <a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed">
         <span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (December 2011)">
          citation needed
         </span>
        </a>
       </i>
       ]
      </sup>
      An example of this type can be found within the
      <i>
       <a href="/wiki/Lampropholis_delicata" title="Lampropholis delicata">
        Lampropholis delicata
       </a>
      </i>
      species of skink.
     </p>
     <p>
      Ecosystem changes can alter species' distributions. For example,
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Edge_effect" title="Edge effect">
       edge effects
      </a>
      describe what happens when part of an ecosystem is disturbed as when land is cleared for agriculture. The boundary between remaining undisturbed habitat and the newly cleared land itself forms a distinct habitat, creating new winners and losers and possibly hosting species that would not thrive outside the boundary habitat.
      <sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">
       [
       <i>
        <a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed">
         <span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (December 2011)">
          citation needed
         </span>
        </a>
       </i>
       ]
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      One interesting finding in studies of invasive species has shown that introduced populations have great potential for rapid adaptation and this is used to explain how so many introduced species are able to establish and become invasive in new environments. When bottlenecks and founder effects cause a great decrease in the population size and may constrict genetic variation,
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-25">
       <a href="#cite_note-25">
        [25]
       </a>
      </sup>
      the individuals begin to show additive variance as opposed to epistatic variance. This conversion can actually lead to increased variance in the founding populations which then allows for rapid adaptive evolution.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Prentis_2008_288-294_26-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-Prentis_2008_288-294-26">
        [26]
       </a>
      </sup>
      Following invasion events, selection may initially act on the capacity to disperse as well as physiological tolerance to the new stressors in the environment. Adaptation then proceeds to respond to the selective pressures of the new environment. These responses would most likely be due to temperature and climate change, or the presence of native species whether it be predator or prey.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Eunmi_2002_386-391_27-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-Eunmi_2002_386-391-27">
        [27]
       </a>
      </sup>
      Adaptations include changes in morphology, physiology, phenology, and plasticity.
     </p>
     <p>
      Rapid adaptive evolution in these species leads to offspring that have higher fitness and are better suited for their environment. Intraspecific phenotypic plasticity, pre-adaptation and post-introduction evolution are all major factors in adaptive evolution.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Zenni_2013_635-644_28-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-Zenni_2013_635-644-28">
        [28]
       </a>
      </sup>
      Plasticity in populations allows room for changes to better suit the individual in its environment. This is key in adaptive evolution because the main goal is how to best be suited to the ecosystem that the species has been introduced. The ability to accomplish this as quickly as possible will lead to a population with a very high fitness. Pre-adaptations and evolution after the initial introduction also play a role in the success of the introduced species. If the species has adapted to a similar ecosystem or contains traits that happen to be well suited to the area that it is introduced, it is more likely to fare better in the new environment. This, in addition to evolution that takes place after introduction, all determine if the species will be able to become established in the new ecosystem and if it will reproduce and thrive.
     </p>
     <h2>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="Ecology">
       Ecology
      </span>
      <span class="mw-editsection">
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        [
       </span>
       <a href="/w/index.php?title=Invasive_species&amp;action=edit&amp;section=4" title="Edit section: Ecology">
        edit
       </a>
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        ]
       </span>
      </span>
     </h2>
     <h3>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="Traits_of_invaded_ecosystems">
       Traits of invaded ecosystems
      </span>
      <span class="mw-editsection">
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        [
       </span>
       <a href="/w/index.php?title=Invasive_species&amp;action=edit&amp;section=5" title="Edit section: Traits of invaded ecosystems">
        edit
       </a>
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        ]
       </span>
      </span>
     </h3>
     <p>
      In 1958, Charles S. Elton
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-elton_29-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-elton-29">
        [29]
       </a>
      </sup>
      claimed that ecosystems with higher
      <a href="/wiki/Species_diversity" title="Species diversity">
       species diversity
      </a>
      were less subject to invasive species because of fewer available niches. Other ecologists later pointed to highly diverse, but heavily invaded ecosystems and argued that ecosystems with high species diversity were more susceptible to invasion.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-autogenerated2_30-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-autogenerated2-30">
        [30]
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      This debate hinged on the
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Scale_(spatial)" title="Scale (spatial)">
       spatial scale
      </a>
      at which invasion studies were performed, and the issue of how diversity affects susceptibility remained unresolved as of 2011. Small-scale studies tended to show a negative relationship between diversity and invasion, while large-scale studies tended to show the reverse. The latter result may be a side-effect of invasives' ability to capitalize on increased resource availability and weaker species interactions that are more common when larger samples are considered.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-31">
       <a href="#cite_note-31">
        [31]
       </a>
      </sup>
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-32">
       <a href="#cite_note-32">
        [32]
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <div class="thumb tright">
      <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
       <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Snake_browntree.jpg">
        <img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="481" data-file-width="608" height="174" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d2/Snake_browntree.jpg/220px-Snake_browntree.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d2/Snake_browntree.jpg/330px-Snake_browntree.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d2/Snake_browntree.jpg/440px-Snake_browntree.jpg 2x" width="220"/>
       </a>
       <div class="thumbcaption">
        <div class="magnify">
         <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Snake_browntree.jpg" title="Enlarge">
         </a>
        </div>
        The
        <a href="/wiki/Brown_tree_snake" title="Brown tree snake">
         brown tree snake
        </a>
        (
        <i>
         Boiga irregularis
        </i>
        )
       </div>
      </div>
     </div>
     <p>
      Invasion was more likely in ecosystems that were similar to the one in which the potential invader evolved.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-autogenerated1_33-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-autogenerated1-33">
        [33]
       </a>
      </sup>
      Island ecosystems may be more prone to invasion because their species faced few strong competitors and predators, or because their distance from colonizing species populations makes them more likely to have "open" niches.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-stach_34-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-stach-34">
        [34]
       </a>
      </sup>
      An example of this phenomenon was the decimation of native bird populations on
      <a href="/wiki/Guam" title="Guam">
       Guam
      </a>
      by the invasive
      <a href="/wiki/Brown_tree_snake" title="Brown tree snake">
       brown tree snake
      </a>
      .
      <sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">
       [
       <i>
        <a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed">
         <span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (July 2015)">
          citation needed
         </span>
        </a>
       </i>
       ]
      </sup>
      Conversely, invaded ecosystems may lack the natural competitors and predators that check invasives' growth in their native ecosystems.
     </p>
     <p>
      Invaded ecosystems may have experienced disturbance, typically human-induced.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-williams_11-1">
       <a href="#cite_note-williams-11">
        [11]
       </a>
      </sup>
      Such a disturbance may give invasive species a chance to establish themselves with less competition from natives less able to adapt to a disturbed ecosystem.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-tilm_13-1">
       <a href="#cite_note-tilm-13">
        [13]
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <h3>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="Vectors">
       Vectors
      </span>
      <span class="mw-editsection">
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        [
       </span>
       <a href="/w/index.php?title=Invasive_species&amp;action=edit&amp;section=6" title="Edit section: Vectors">
        edit
       </a>
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        ]
       </span>
      </span>
     </h3>
     <p>
      Non-native species have many
      <i>
       <a href="/wiki/Vector_(epidemiology)" title="Vector (epidemiology)">
        vectors
       </a>
      </i>
      , including biogenic vectors, but most invasions are associated with human activity. Natural
      <a href="/wiki/Range_(biology)" title="Range (biology)">
       range
      </a>
      extensions are common in many species, but the rate and magnitude of human-mediated extensions in these species tend to be much larger than natural extensions, and humans typically carry specimens greater distances than natural forces.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-35">
       <a href="#cite_note-35">
        [35]
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      An early human vector occurred when prehistoric humans introduced the
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Pacific_rat" title="Pacific rat">
       Pacific rat
      </a>
      (
      <i>
       Rattus exulans
      </i>
      ) to Polynesia.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-36">
       <a href="#cite_note-36">
        [36]
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <div class="thumb tleft">
      <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
       <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:EriocheirSinensis1.jpg">
        <img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="876" data-file-width="1347" height="143" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/70/EriocheirSinensis1.jpg/220px-EriocheirSinensis1.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/70/EriocheirSinensis1.jpg/330px-EriocheirSinensis1.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/70/EriocheirSinensis1.jpg/440px-EriocheirSinensis1.jpg 2x" width="220"/>
       </a>
       <div class="thumbcaption">
        <div class="magnify">
         <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:EriocheirSinensis1.jpg" title="Enlarge">
         </a>
        </div>
        <a href="/wiki/Chinese_mitten_crab" title="Chinese mitten crab">
         Chinese mitten crab
        </a>
        (
        <i>
         Eriocheir sinensis
        </i>
        )
       </div>
      </div>
     </div>
     <p>
      Vectors include plants or seeds imported for
      <a href="/wiki/Horticulture" title="Horticulture">
       horticulture
      </a>
      . The
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Pet_trade" title="Pet trade">
       pet trade
      </a>
      moves animals across borders, where they can escape and become invasive. Organisms stow away on transport vehicles.
      <a href="/wiki/Ballast_water_discharge_and_the_environment" title="Ballast water discharge and the environment">
       Ballast water
      </a>
      taken up at sea and released in port by transoceanic vessels is the largest vector for non-native aquatic species invasions.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-MolnarGamboa2008_37-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-MolnarGamboa2008-37">
        [37]
       </a>
      </sup>
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-XuWickramarathne2014_38-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-XuWickramarathne2014-38">
        [38]
       </a>
      </sup>
      Around the world on the average day, more than 3,000 different species of aquatic life may be transported on these vessels.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-39">
       <a href="#cite_note-39">
        [39]
       </a>
      </sup>
      For example, freshwater
      <a href="/wiki/Zebra_mussel" title="Zebra mussel">
       zebra mussels
      </a>
      , native to the
      <a href="/wiki/Black_Sea" title="Black Sea">
       Black
      </a>
      ,
      <a href="/wiki/Caspian_Sea" title="Caspian Sea">
       Caspian
      </a>
      and
      <a href="/wiki/Sea_of_Azov" title="Sea of Azov">
       Azov seas
      </a>
      , probably reached the
      <a href="/wiki/Great_Lakes" title="Great Lakes">
       Great Lakes
      </a>
      via ballast water from a transoceanic vessel.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-40">
       <a href="#cite_note-40">
        [40]
       </a>
      </sup>
      Although the zebra mussel invasion was first noted in 1988, and a mitigation plan was successfully implemented shortly thereafter, the plan had (and continued to have as of 2005) a serious flaw or loophole, whereby ships that are loaded with cargo when they reach the
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/St._Lawrence_Seaway" title="St. Lawrence Seaway">
       Seaway
      </a>
      need not be tested, but all the same they transfer ballast 'puddles' between Seaway ports.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-41">
       <a href="#cite_note-41">
        [41]
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      The arrival of invasive
      <a href="/wiki/Propagule" title="Propagule">
       propagules
      </a>
      to a new site is a function of the site's invasibility.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-42">
       <a href="#cite_note-42">
        [42]
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      Species have also been introduced intentionally. For example, to feel more "at home," American colonists formed "Acclimation Societies" that repeatedly imported birds that were native to Europe to North America and other distant lands. In 2008, U.S. postal workers in Pennsylvania noticed noises coming from inside a box from Taiwan; the box contained more than two dozen live beetles.
      <a href="/wiki/Agricultural_Research_Service" title="Agricultural Research Service">
       Agricultural Research Service
      </a>
      entomologists identified them as
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Rhinoceros_beetle" title="Rhinoceros beetle">
       rhinoceros beetle
      </a>
      ,
      <a href="/wiki/Hercules_beetle" title="Hercules beetle">
       hercules beetle
      </a>
      , and
      <a href="/wiki/Phalacrognathus_muelleri" title="Phalacrognathus muelleri">
       king stag beetle
      </a>
      .
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-43">
       <a href="#cite_note-43">
        [43]
       </a>
      </sup>
      Because these species were not native to the U.S., they could have threatened native ecosystems. To prevent exotic species from becoming a problem in the U.S., special handling and permits are required when living materials are shipped from foreign countries.
      <a href="/wiki/United_States_Department_of_Agriculture" title="United States Department of Agriculture">
       USDA
      </a>
      programs such as
      <a href="/wiki/Smuggling_Interdiction_and_Trade_Compliance" title="Smuggling Interdiction and Trade Compliance">
       Smuggling Interdiction and Trade Compliance
      </a>
      (SITC) attempt to prevent exotic species outbreaks in America.
     </p>
     <p>
      Economics plays a major role in exotic species introduction. High demand for the valuable
      <a href="/wiki/Chinese_mitten_crab" title="Chinese mitten crab">
       Chinese mitten crab
      </a>
      is one explanation for the possible intentional release of the species in foreign waters.
      <sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">
       [
       <i>
        <a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed">
         <span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (December 2011)">
          citation needed
         </span>
        </a>
       </i>
       ]
      </sup>
     </p>
     <h3>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="Impacts_of_wildfire">
       Impacts of wildfire
      </span>
      <span class="mw-editsection">
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        [
       </span>
       <a href="/w/index.php?title=Invasive_species&amp;action=edit&amp;section=7" title="Edit section: Impacts of wildfire">
        edit
       </a>
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        ]
       </span>
      </span>
     </h3>
     <p>
      Invasive species often exploit disturbances to an ecosystem (
      <a href="/wiki/Wildfire" title="Wildfire">
       wildfires
      </a>
      , roads, foot trails) to colonize an area. Large wildfires can sterilize soils, while adding a variety of nutrients.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Davis_2000_528.E2.80.93534_24-1">
       <a href="#cite_note-Davis_2000_528.E2.80.93534-24">
        [24]
       </a>
      </sup>
      In the resulting free-for-all, formerly entrenched species lose their advantage, leaving more room for invasives. In such circumstances plants that can regenerate from their roots have an advantage. Non-natives with this ability can benefit from a low intensity fire burns that removes surface vegetation, leaving natives that rely on seeds for propagation to find their niches occupied when their seeds finally sprout.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Brooks_2004_677.E2.80.93688_21-1">
       <a href="#cite_note-Brooks_2004_677.E2.80.93688-21">
        [21]
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <h4>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="Impact_of_wildfire_suppression_on_spreading">
       Impact of wildfire suppression on spreading
      </span>
      <span class="mw-editsection">
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        [
       </span>
       <a href="/w/index.php?title=Invasive_species&amp;action=edit&amp;section=8" title="Edit section: Impact of wildfire suppression on spreading">
        edit
       </a>
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        ]
       </span>
      </span>
     </h4>
     <p>
      Wildfires often occur in remote areas, needing fire suppression crews to travel through pristine forest to reach the site. The crews can bring invasive seeds with them. If any of these stowaway seeds become established, a thriving colony of invasives can erupt in as few as six weeks, after which controlling the outbreak can need years of continued attention to prevent further spread. Also, disturbing the soil surface, such as cutting firebreaks, destroys native cover, exposes soil, and can accelerate invasions. In suburban and
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Wildland-urban_interface" title="Wildland-urban interface">
       wildland-urban interface
      </a>
      areas, the vegetation clearance and brush removal ordinances of municipalities for
      <a href="/wiki/Defensible_space_(fire_control)" title="Defensible space (fire control)">
       defensible space
      </a>
      can result in excessive removal of native shrubs and perennials that exposes the soil to more light and less competition for invasive plant species.
      <sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">
       [
       <i>
        <a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed">
         <span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (December 2011)">
          citation needed
         </span>
        </a>
       </i>
       ]
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      Fire suppression vehicles are often major culprits in such outbreaks, as the vehicles are often driven on back roads often overgrown with invasive plant species. The undercarriage of the vehicle becomes a prime vessel of transport. In response, on large fires, washing stations "decontaminate" vehicles before engaging in suppression activities.
      <sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">
       [
       <i>
        <a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed">
         <span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (December 2011)">
          citation needed
         </span>
        </a>
       </i>
       ]
      </sup>
      Large wildfires attract firefighters from remote places, further increasing the potential for seed transport.
      <sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">
       [
       <i>
        <a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed">
         <span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (December 2011)">
          citation needed
         </span>
        </a>
       </i>
       ]
      </sup>
     </p>
     <h2>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="Effects">
       Effects
      </span>
      <span class="mw-editsection">
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        [
       </span>
       <a href="/w/index.php?title=Invasive_species&amp;action=edit&amp;section=9" title="Edit section: Effects">
        edit
       </a>
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        ]
       </span>
      </span>
     </h2>
     <div class="thumb tright">
      <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
       <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Gator_and_Python.jpg">
        <img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1282" data-file-width="1710" height="165" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3e/Gator_and_Python.jpg/220px-Gator_and_Python.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3e/Gator_and_Python.jpg/330px-Gator_and_Python.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3e/Gator_and_Python.jpg/440px-Gator_and_Python.jpg 2x" width="220"/>
       </a>
       <div class="thumbcaption">
        <div class="magnify">
         <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Gator_and_Python.jpg" title="Enlarge">
         </a>
        </div>
        An
        <a href="/wiki/American_alligator" title="American alligator">
         American alligator
        </a>
        attacking a
        <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Burmese_python_in_Florida" title="Burmese python in Florida">
         Burmese python in Florida
        </a>
        ; the
        <a href="/wiki/Burmese_python" title="Burmese python">
         Burmese python
        </a>
        is an invasive species which is posing a threat to many indigenous species, including the alligator
       </div>
      </div>
     </div>
     <h3>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="Ecological">
       Ecological
      </span>
      <span class="mw-editsection">
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        [
       </span>
       <a href="/w/index.php?title=Invasive_species&amp;action=edit&amp;section=10" title="Edit section: Ecological">
        edit
       </a>
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        ]
       </span>
      </span>
     </h3>
     <p>
      Land clearing and human habitation put significant pressure on local species. Disturbed habitats are prone to invasions that can have adverse effects on local ecosystems, changing ecosystem functions. A species of wetland plant known as
      <span class="unicode">
       ʻ
      </span>
      ae
      <span class="unicode">
       ʻ
      </span>
      ae in Hawaii (the indigenous
      <i>
       <a href="/wiki/Bacopa_monnieri" title="Bacopa monnieri">
        Bacopa monnieri
       </a>
      </i>
      ) is regarded as a pest species in artificially manipulated water bird refuges because it quickly covers shallow mudflats established for endangered Hawaiian stilt (
      <i>
       <a href="/wiki/Black-winged_stilt" title="Black-winged stilt">
        Himantopus mexicanus knudseni
       </a>
      </i>
      ), making these undesirable feeding areas for the birds.
     </p>
     <p>
      Multiple successive introductions of different non-native species can have interactive effects; the introduction of a second non-native species can enable the first invasive species to flourish. Examples of this are the introductions of the
      <a href="/wiki/Amethyst_gem_clam" title="Amethyst gem clam">
       amethyst gem clam
      </a>
      (
      <i>
       Gemma gemma
      </i>
      ) and the European green crab (
      <i>
       <a href="/wiki/Carcinus_maenas" title="Carcinus maenas">
        Carcinus maenas
       </a>
      </i>
      ). The gem clam was introduced into California's
      <a href="/wiki/Bodega_Bay" title="Bodega Bay">
       Bodega Harbor
      </a>
      from the East Coast of the United States a century ago. It had been found in small quantities in the harbor but had never displaced the native clam species (
      <i>
       Nutricola
      </i>
      spp.). In the mid-1990s, the introduction of the European green crab, found to prey preferentially on the native clams, resulted in a decline of the native clams and an increase of the introduced clam populations.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-44">
       <a href="#cite_note-44">
        [44]
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      In the
      <a href="/wiki/Waterberg_Biosphere" title="Waterberg Biosphere">
       Waterberg
      </a>
      region of
      <a href="/wiki/South_Africa" title="South Africa">
       South Africa
      </a>
      , cattle grazing over the past six centuries has allowed invasive scrub and small trees to displace much of the original
      <a href="/wiki/Grassland" title="Grassland">
       grassland
      </a>
      , resulting in a massive reduction in
      <a href="/wiki/Fodder" title="Fodder">
       forage
      </a>
      for native bovids and other grazers. Since the 1970s, large scale efforts have been underway to reduce invasive species; partial success has led to re-establishment of many species that had dwindled or left the region. Examples of these species are
      <a href="/wiki/Giraffe" title="Giraffe">
       giraffe
      </a>
      ,
      <a href="/wiki/Blue_wildebeest" title="Blue wildebeest">
       blue wildebeest
      </a>
      ,
      <a href="/wiki/Impala" title="Impala">
       impala
      </a>
      ,
      <a href="/wiki/Kudu" title="Kudu">
       kudu
      </a>
      and
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/White_rhino" title="White rhino">
       white rhino
      </a>
      .
     </p>
     <p>
      Invasive species can change the functions of ecosystems. For example, invasive plants can alter the fire regimen (cheatgrass,
      <i>
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Drooping_Brome" title="Drooping Brome">
        Bromus tectorum
       </a>
      </i>
      ), nutrient cycling (smooth cordgrass
      <i>
       <a href="/wiki/Spartina_alterniflora" title="Spartina alterniflora">
        Spartina alterniflora
       </a>
      </i>
      ), and hydrology (
      <i>
       <a href="/wiki/Tamarix" title="Tamarix">
        Tamarix
       </a>
      </i>
      ) in native ecosystems.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-45">
       <a href="#cite_note-45">
        [45]
       </a>
      </sup>
      Invasive species that are closely related to rare native species have the potential to hybridize with the native species. Harmful effects of hybridization have led to a decline and even extinction of native species.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-46">
       <a href="#cite_note-46">
        [46]
       </a>
      </sup>
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-rhymer_47-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-rhymer-47">
        [47]
       </a>
      </sup>
      For example, hybridization with introduced cordgrass,
      <i>
       Spartina alterniflora
      </i>
      , threatens the existence of California cordgrass (
      <i>
       <a href="/wiki/Spartina_foliosa" title="Spartina foliosa">
        Spartina foliosa
       </a>
      </i>
      ) in San Francisco Bay.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ayres_48-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-ayres-48">
        [48]
       </a>
      </sup>
      Invasive species cause competition for native species and because of this 400 of the 958 endangered species under the Endangered Species Act are at risk
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-49">
       <a href="#cite_note-49">
        [49]
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <h3>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="Geomorphological">
       Geomorphological
      </span>
      <span class="mw-editsection">
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        [
       </span>
       <a href="/w/index.php?title=Invasive_species&amp;action=edit&amp;section=11" title="Edit section: Geomorphological">
        edit
       </a>
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        ]
       </span>
      </span>
     </h3>
     <p>
      Primary geomorphological effects of invasive plants are bioconstruction and bioprotection. For example, Kudzu
      <a href="/wiki/Pueraria_montana" title="Pueraria montana">
       Pueraria montana
      </a>
      , a vine native to Asia was widely introduced in the southeastern USA in the early 20th century to control soil erosion. While primary effects of invasive animals are bioturbation, bioerosion, and bioconstruction. For example, invasion of Chinese mitten crab
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Eriocheir_sinensis" title="Eriocheir sinensis">
       Eriocheir sinensis
      </a>
      have resulted in higher bioturbation and bioerosion rates.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-50">
       <a href="#cite_note-50">
        [50]
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <h3>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="Economic">
       Economic
      </span>
      <span class="mw-editsection">
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        [
       </span>
       <a href="/w/index.php?title=Invasive_species&amp;action=edit&amp;section=12" title="Edit section: Economic">
        edit
       </a>
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        ]
       </span>
      </span>
     </h3>
     <h4>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="Economic_opportunities">
       Economic opportunities
      </span>
      <span class="mw-editsection">
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        [
       </span>
       <a href="/w/index.php?title=Invasive_species&amp;action=edit&amp;section=13" title="Edit section: Economic opportunities">
        edit
       </a>
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        ]
       </span>
      </span>
     </h4>
     <p>
      Some invasions offer potential commercial benefits. For instance,
      <a href="/wiki/Silver_carp" title="Silver carp">
       silver carp
      </a>
      and
      <a href="/wiki/Common_carp" title="Common carp">
       common carp
      </a>
      can be harvested for human food and exported to markets already familiar with the product, or processed into
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Pet_foods" title="Pet foods">
       pet foods
      </a>
      , or
      <a href="/wiki/Mink" title="Mink">
       mink
      </a>
      feed.
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Water_hyacinth" title="Water hyacinth">
       Water hyacinth
      </a>
      can be turned into fuel by
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Methane_digesters" title="Methane digesters">
       methane digesters
      </a>
      ,
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-wolv_51-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-wolv-51">
        [51]
       </a>
      </sup>
      and other invasive plants can also be harvested and utilized as a source of
      <a href="/wiki/Bioenergy" title="Bioenergy">
       bioenergy
      </a>
      .
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-52">
       <a href="#cite_note-52">
        [52]
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <h5>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="Benefits">
       Benefits
      </span>
      <span class="mw-editsection">
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        [
       </span>
       <a href="/w/index.php?title=Invasive_species&amp;action=edit&amp;section=14" title="Edit section: Benefits">
        edit
       </a>
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        ]
       </span>
      </span>
     </h5>
     <p>
      Non-native species can have benefits.
      <a href="/wiki/Chesapeake_Bay#Depletion_of_oysters" title="Chesapeake Bay">
       Asian oysters
      </a>
      , for example, filter water pollutants better than native
      <sup class="noprint Inline-Template" style="margin-left:0.1em; white-space:nowrap;">
       [
       <i>
        <a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Please_clarify" title="Wikipedia:Please clarify">
         <span title="native to where? (September 2012)">
          clarification needed
         </span>
        </a>
       </i>
       ]
      </sup>
      oysters. They also grow faster and withstand disease better than natives. Biologists are currently considering releasing this
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Mollusk" title="Mollusk">
       mollusk
      </a>
      in the
      <a href="/wiki/Chesapeake_Bay" title="Chesapeake Bay">
       Chesapeake Bay
      </a>
      to help restore oyster stocks and remove pollution. A recent study by the
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Johns_Hopkins_School_of_Public_Health" title="Johns Hopkins School of Public Health">
       Johns Hopkins School of Public Health
      </a>
      found the Asian oyster could significantly benefit the bay's deteriorating water quality.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-53">
       <a href="#cite_note-53">
        [53]
       </a>
      </sup>
      Additionally, some species have invaded an area so long ago that they have found their own beneficial niche in the environment. For example,
      <i>
       <a href="/wiki/Lasioglossum_leucozonium" title="Lasioglossum leucozonium">
        L. leucozonium
       </a>
      </i>
      , shown by population genetic analysis to be an invasive species in North America,
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-54">
       <a href="#cite_note-54">
        [54]
       </a>
      </sup>
      has become an important pollinator of
      <a href="/wiki/Rubus" title="Rubus">
       caneberry
      </a>
      as well as
      <a href="/wiki/Cucurbitaceae" title="Cucurbitaceae">
       cucurbit
      </a>
      ,
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Apple_trees" title="Apple trees">
       apple trees
      </a>
      , and blueberry bushes.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-An_Assessment_of_Non-Apis_Bees_as_Fruit_and_Vegetable_Crop_Pollinators_in_Southwest_Virginia_55-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-An_Assessment_of_Non-Apis_Bees_as_Fruit_and_Vegetable_Crop_Pollinators_in_Southwest_Virginia-55">
        [55]
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <h5>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="Invasivorism">
       Invasivorism
      </span>
      <span class="mw-editsection">
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        [
       </span>
       <a href="/w/index.php?title=Invasive_species&amp;action=edit&amp;section=15" title="Edit section: Invasivorism">
        edit
       </a>
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        ]
       </span>
      </span>
     </h5>
     <p>
      Invasive species are flora and fauna whose introduction into a habitat disrupts the native eco-system. In response, Invasivorism is a movement that explores the idea of eating invasive species in order to control, reduce, or eliminate their populations. Chefs from around the world have begun seeking out and using invasive species as alternative ingredients.
      <a href="/wiki/Miya%27s" title="Miya's">
       Miya's
      </a>
      of
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/New_Haven" title="New Haven">
       New Haven
      </a>
      ,
      <a href="/wiki/Connecticut" title="Connecticut">
       Connecticut
      </a>
      created the first invasive species menu in the world.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-56">
       <a href="#cite_note-56">
        [56]
       </a>
      </sup>
      Skeptics point out that once a foreign species has entrenched itself in a new place—such as the Indo-Pacific lionfish that has now virtually taken over the waters of the Western Atlantic, Caribbean and Gulf of Mexico—eradication is almost impossible. Critics argue that encouraging consumption might have the unintended effect of spreading harmful species even more widely.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-57">
       <a href="#cite_note-57">
        [57]
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <div class="thumb tright">
      <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
       <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:A_dish_that_features_whole_fried_invasive_lionfish_at_Fish_Fish_of_Miami,_Florida.jpg">
        <img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="519" data-file-width="800" height="143" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/39/A_dish_that_features_whole_fried_invasive_lionfish_at_Fish_Fish_of_Miami%2C_Florida.jpg/220px-A_dish_that_features_whole_fried_invasive_lionfish_at_Fish_Fish_of_Miami%2C_Florida.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/39/A_dish_that_features_whole_fried_invasive_lionfish_at_Fish_Fish_of_Miami%2C_Florida.jpg/330px-A_dish_that_features_whole_fried_invasive_lionfish_at_Fish_Fish_of_Miami%2C_Florida.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/39/A_dish_that_features_whole_fried_invasive_lionfish_at_Fish_Fish_of_Miami%2C_Florida.jpg/440px-A_dish_that_features_whole_fried_invasive_lionfish_at_Fish_Fish_of_Miami%2C_Florida.jpg 2x" width="220"/>
       </a>
       <div class="thumbcaption">
        <div class="magnify">
         <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:A_dish_that_features_whole_fried_invasive_lionfish_at_Fish_Fish_of_Miami,_Florida.jpg" title="Enlarge">
         </a>
        </div>
        A dish that features whole fried invasive lionfish at Fish Fish of Miami, Florida
       </div>
      </div>
     </div>
     <p>
      Proponents of invasivorism argue that humans have the ability to eat away any species that it has an appetite for, pointing to the many animals which humans have been able to hunt to extinction - such as the
      <a href="/wiki/Dodo" title="Dodo">
       Dodo
      </a>
      bird, the
      <a href="/wiki/Caribbean_monk_seal" title="Caribbean monk seal">
       Caribbean monk seal
      </a>
      , and the
      <a href="/wiki/Passenger_pigeon" title="Passenger pigeon">
       Passenger pigeon
      </a>
      . Proponents of invasivorism also point to the success that Jamaica has had in significantly decreasing the population of
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Lionfish" title="Lionfish">
       lionfish
      </a>
      by encouraging the consumption of the fish.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-58">
       <a href="#cite_note-58">
        [58]
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <h4>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="Costs">
       Costs
      </span>
      <span class="mw-editsection">
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        [
       </span>
       <a href="/w/index.php?title=Invasive_species&amp;action=edit&amp;section=16" title="Edit section: Costs">
        edit
       </a>
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        ]
       </span>
      </span>
     </h4>
     <p>
      Economic costs from invasive species can be separated into direct costs through production loss in agriculture and forestry, and management costs. Estimated damage and control cost of invasive species in the U.S. alone amount to more than $138 billion annually.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-pimental_59-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-pimental-59">
        [59]
       </a>
      </sup>
      Economic losses can also occur through loss of recreational and tourism revenues.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-simb_60-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-simb-60">
        [60]
       </a>
      </sup>
      When economic costs of invasions are calculated as production loss and management costs, they are low because they do not consider environmental damage; if monetary values were assigned to the extinction of species, loss in biodiversity, and loss of
      <a href="/wiki/Ecosystem_services" title="Ecosystem services">
       ecosystem services
      </a>
      , costs from impacts of invasive species would drastically increase.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-pimental_59-1">
       <a href="#cite_note-pimental-59">
        [59]
       </a>
      </sup>
      The following examples from different sectors of the economy demonstrate the impact of biological invasions.
     </p>
     <h4>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="Plant_industry">
       Plant industry
      </span>
      <span class="mw-editsection">
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        [
       </span>
       <a href="/w/index.php?title=Invasive_species&amp;action=edit&amp;section=17" title="Edit section: Plant industry">
        edit
       </a>
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        ]
       </span>
      </span>
     </h4>
     <p>
      Weeds reduce yield in agriculture, though they may provide essential nutrients. Some deep-rooted weeds can "mine" nutrients (see
      <a href="/wiki/Dynamic_accumulator" title="Dynamic accumulator">
       dynamic accumulator
      </a>
      ) from the subsoil and deposit them on the topsoil, while others provide habitat for beneficial insects or provide foods for pest species. Many weed species are accidental introductions that accompany seeds and imported plant material. Many introduced weeds in pastures compete with native forage plants, threaten young cattle (e.g., leafy spurge,
      <i>
       <a href="/wiki/Euphorbia_esula" title="Euphorbia esula">
        Euphorbia esula
       </a>
      </i>
      ) or are unpalatable because of thorns and spines (e.g.,
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Yellow_starthistle" title="Yellow starthistle">
       yellow starthistle
      </a>
      ). Forage loss from invasive weeds on pastures amounts to nearly
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/US$" title="US$">
       US$
      </a>
      1 billion in the U.S. alone.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-pimental_59-2">
       <a href="#cite_note-pimental-59">
        [59]
       </a>
      </sup>
      A decline in pollinator services and loss of fruit production has been caused by
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Western_honeybee" title="Western honeybee">
       honey bees
      </a>
      infected by the invasive
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Varroa_mite" title="Varroa mite">
       varroa mite
      </a>
      . Introduced rats (
      <i>
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Rattus_rattus" title="Rattus rattus">
        Rattus rattus
       </a>
      </i>
      and
      <i>
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Rattus_norvegicus" title="Rattus norvegicus">
        R. norvegicus
       </a>
      </i>
      ) have become serious pests on farms, destroying stored grains.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-pimental_59-3">
       <a href="#cite_note-pimental-59">
        [59]
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      Invasive plant pathogens and insect vectors for plant diseases can also suppress agricultural yields and nursery stock. Citrus greening is a bacterial disease vectored by the invasive Asian citrus psyllid (ACP). Because of the impacts of this disease on citrus crops, citrus is under quarantine and highly regulated in areas where ACP has been found.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-61">
       <a href="#cite_note-61">
        [61]
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <h4>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="Aquaculture">
       Aquaculture
      </span>
      <span class="mw-editsection">
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        [
       </span>
       <a href="/w/index.php?title=Invasive_species&amp;action=edit&amp;section=18" title="Edit section: Aquaculture">
        edit
       </a>
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        ]
       </span>
      </span>
     </h4>
     <p>
      <a href="/wiki/Aquaculture" title="Aquaculture">
       Aquaculture
      </a>
      is a very common vector of species introductions – mainly of species with economic potential (e.g.,
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Oreochromis_niloticus" title="Oreochromis niloticus">
       Oreochromis niloticus
      </a>
      )
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-62">
       <a href="#cite_note-62">
        [62]
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <h4>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="Forestry">
       Forestry
      </span>
      <span class="mw-editsection">
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        [
       </span>
       <a href="/w/index.php?title=Invasive_species&amp;action=edit&amp;section=19" title="Edit section: Forestry">
        edit
       </a>
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        ]
       </span>
      </span>
     </h4>
     <div class="hatnote" role="note">
      See also:
      <a href="/wiki/Wilding_conifer" title="Wilding conifer">
       Wilding conifer
      </a>
     </div>
     <div class="thumb tright">
      <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
       <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Firewoodposter_white_web.pdf">
        <img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="7447" data-file-width="5885" height="278" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/74/Firewoodposter_white_web.pdf/page1-220px-Firewoodposter_white_web.pdf.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/74/Firewoodposter_white_web.pdf/page1-330px-Firewoodposter_white_web.pdf.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/74/Firewoodposter_white_web.pdf/page1-440px-Firewoodposter_white_web.pdf.jpg 2x" width="220"/>
       </a>
       <div class="thumbcaption">
        <div class="magnify">
         <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Firewoodposter_white_web.pdf" title="Enlarge">
         </a>
        </div>
        Poster asking campers to not move firewood around, avoiding the spread of invasive species.
       </div>
      </div>
     </div>
     <p>
      The unintentional introduction of forest pest species and plant pathogens can change
      <a href="/wiki/Forest_ecology" title="Forest ecology">
       forest ecology
      </a>
      and damage the timber industry. Overall, forest ecosystems in the U.S. are widely invaded by exotic pests, plants, and pathogens.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-63">
       <a href="#cite_note-63">
        [63]
       </a>
      </sup>
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-64">
       <a href="#cite_note-64">
        [64]
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      The Asian long-horned beetle (
      <i>
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Anoplophora_glabripennis" title="Anoplophora glabripennis">
        Anoplophora glabripennis
       </a>
      </i>
      ) was first introduced into the U.S. in 1996, and was expected to infect and damage millions of acres of hardwood trees. As of 2005 thirty million dollars had been spent in attempts to eradicate this pest and protect millions of trees in the affected regions.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-pimental_59-4">
       <a href="#cite_note-pimental-59">
        [59]
       </a>
      </sup>
      The
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Woolly_adelgid" title="Woolly adelgid">
       woolly adelgid
      </a>
      has inflicted damage on old-growth spruce, fir and
      <a href="/wiki/Tsuga" title="Tsuga">
       hemlock
      </a>
      forests and damages the
      <a href="/wiki/Christmas_tree" title="Christmas tree">
       Christmas tree
      </a>
      industry.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-65">
       <a href="#cite_note-65">
        [65]
       </a>
      </sup>
      And the chestnut blight fungus (
      <i>
       <a href="/wiki/Chestnut_blight" title="Chestnut blight">
        Cryphonectria parasitica
       </a>
      </i>
      ) and Dutch elm disease (
      <i>
       <a href="/wiki/Dutch_elm_disease" title="Dutch elm disease">
