        Ophiostoma novo-ulmi
       </a>
      </i>
      ) are two plant pathogens with serious impacts on these two species, and forest health.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-USDA-Forest_Service-Schlarbaum-1997_66-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-USDA-Forest_Service-Schlarbaum-1997-66">
        [66]
       </a>
      </sup>
      Garlic mustard,
      <i>
       <a href="/wiki/Alliaria_petiolata" title="Alliaria petiolata">
        Alliaria petiolata
       </a>
      </i>
      , is one of the most problematic invasive plant species in eastern North American forests. The characteristics of garlic mustard are slightly different from those of the surrounding native plants, which results in a highly successful species that is altering the composition and function of the native communities it invades. When garlic mustard invades the understory of a forest, it affects the growth rate of tree seedlings, which is likely to alter forest regeneration of impact forest composition in the future.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-67">
       <a href="#cite_note-67">
        [67]
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <h4>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="Tourism_and_recreation">
       Tourism and recreation
      </span>
      <span class="mw-editsection">
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        [
       </span>
       <a href="/w/index.php?title=Invasive_species&amp;action=edit&amp;section=20" title="Edit section: Tourism and recreation">
        edit
       </a>
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        ]
       </span>
      </span>
     </h4>
     <p>
      Invasive species can impact outdoor recreation, such as fishing, hunting, hiking, wildlife viewing, and water-based activities. They can damage a wide array of environmental services that are important to recreation, including, but not limited to, water quality and quantity, plant and animal diversity, and species abundance.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-68">
       <a href="#cite_note-68">
        [68]
       </a>
      </sup>
      Eiswerth states, "very little research has been performed to estimate the corresponding economic losses at spatial scales such as regions, states, and watersheds." Eurasian watermilfoil (
      <i>
       <a href="/wiki/Myriophyllum_spicatum" title="Myriophyllum spicatum">
        Myriophyllum spicatum
       </a>
      </i>
      ) in parts of the US, fill lakes with plants complicating fishing and boating.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-69">
       <a href="#cite_note-69">
        [69]
       </a>
      </sup>
      The very loud call of the introduced
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Common_coqui" title="Common coqui">
       common coqui
      </a>
      depresses real estate values in affected neighborhoods of
      <a href="/wiki/Hawaii" title="Hawaii">
       Hawaii
      </a>
      .
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-70">
       <a href="#cite_note-70">
        [70]
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <h3>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="Health">
       Health
      </span>
      <span class="mw-editsection">
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        [
       </span>
       <a href="/w/index.php?title=Invasive_species&amp;action=edit&amp;section=21" title="Edit section: Health">
        edit
       </a>
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        ]
       </span>
      </span>
     </h3>
     <p>
      Encroachment of humans into previously remote ecosystems has exposed exotic diseases such as
      <a href="/wiki/HIV" title="HIV">
       HIV
      </a>
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-pimental_59-5">
       <a href="#cite_note-pimental-59">
        [59]
       </a>
      </sup>
      to the wider population. Introduced birds (e.g. pigeons), rodents and insects (e.g. mosquito, flea, louse and
      <a href="/wiki/Tsetse_fly" title="Tsetse fly">
       tsetse fly
      </a>
      pests) can serve as vectors and reservoirs of human afflictions. The introduced Chinese mitten crabs are carriers of
      <a href="/wiki/Paragonimus_westermani" title="Paragonimus westermani">
       Asian lung fluke
      </a>
      .
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-71">
       <a href="#cite_note-71">
        [71]
       </a>
      </sup>
      Throughout recorded history, epidemics of human diseases, such as
      <a href="/wiki/Malaria" title="Malaria">
       malaria
      </a>
      ,
      <a href="/wiki/Yellow_fever" title="Yellow fever">
       yellow fever
      </a>
      ,
      <a href="/wiki/Typhus" title="Typhus">
       typhus
      </a>
      , and
      <a href="/wiki/Bubonic_plague" title="Bubonic plague">
       bubonic plague
      </a>
      , spread via these vectors.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-elton_29-1">
       <a href="#cite_note-elton-29">
        [29]
       </a>
      </sup>
      A recent example of an introduced disease is the spread of the
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/West_Nile_virus" title="West Nile virus">
       West Nile virus
      </a>
      , which killed humans, birds, mammals, and reptiles.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-72">
       <a href="#cite_note-72">
        [72]
       </a>
      </sup>
      Waterborne disease agents, such as
      <a href="/wiki/Cholera" title="Cholera">
       cholera
      </a>
      bacteria (
      <i>
       <a href="/wiki/Vibrio_cholerae" title="Vibrio cholerae">
        Vibrio cholerae
       </a>
      </i>
      ), and causative agents of
      <a href="/wiki/Harmful_algal_bloom" title="Harmful algal bloom">
       harmful algal blooms
      </a>
      are often transported via ballast water.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-73">
       <a href="#cite_note-73">
        [73]
       </a>
      </sup>
      Invasive species and accompanying control efforts can have long term public health implications. For instance, pesticides applied to treat a particular pest species could pollute soil and surface water.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-pimental_59-6">
       <a href="#cite_note-pimental-59">
        [59]
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <h3>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="Biodiversity">
       Biodiversity
      </span>
      <span class="mw-editsection">
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        [
       </span>
       <a href="/w/index.php?title=Invasive_species&amp;action=edit&amp;section=22" title="Edit section: Biodiversity">
        edit
       </a>
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        ]
       </span>
      </span>
     </h3>
     <div class="hatnote" role="note">
      Main article:
      <a href="/wiki/Biodiversity" title="Biodiversity">
       Biodiversity
      </a>
     </div>
     <p>
      Biotic invasion is considered one of the five top drivers for global biodiversity loss and is increasing because of tourism and
      <a href="/wiki/Globalization" title="Globalization">
       globalization
      </a>
      .
      <sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">
       [
       <i>
        <a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed">
         <span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (November 2008)">
          citation needed
         </span>
        </a>
       </i>
       ]
      </sup>
      This may be particularly true in inadequately regulated
      <a href="/wiki/Fresh_water" title="Fresh water">
       fresh water
      </a>
      systems, though
      <a href="/wiki/Quarantine" title="Quarantine">
       quarantines
      </a>
      and
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ballast_water" title="Ballast water">
       ballast water
      </a>
      rules have improved the situation.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-74">
       <a href="#cite_note-74">
        [74]
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      Invasive species may drive local native species to extinction via
      <a href="/wiki/Competition_(biology)" title="Competition (biology)">
       competitive
      </a>
      exclusion,
      <a href="/wiki/Ecological_niche" title="Ecological niche">
       niche
      </a>
      displacement, or
      <a href="/wiki/Hybrid_(biology)" title="Hybrid (biology)">
       hybridisation
      </a>
      with related native species. Therefore, besides their economic ramifications, alien invasions may result in extensive changes in the structure, composition and global distribution of the biota of sites of introduction, leading ultimately to the homogenisation of the world’s fauna and flora and the loss of biodiversity.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Odendaal_2008_75-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-Odendaal_2008-75">
        [75]
       </a>
      </sup>
      Nevertheless, it is difficult to unequivocally attribute extinctions to a species invasion, and the few scientific studies that have done so have been with animal taxa. Concern over the impacts of invasive species on biodiversity must therefore consider the actual evidence (either ecological or economic), in relation to the potential risk.
      <sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">
       [
       <i>
        <a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed">
         <span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (December 2011)">
          citation needed
         </span>
        </a>
       </i>
       ]
      </sup>
     </p>
     <div class="thumb tright">
      <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
       <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Parthenium_smothering_native_flora_in_Biodiversity_Rich_Forest.jpg">
        <img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2592" data-file-width="3456" height="165" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/69/Parthenium_smothering_native_flora_in_Biodiversity_Rich_Forest.jpg/220px-Parthenium_smothering_native_flora_in_Biodiversity_Rich_Forest.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/69/Parthenium_smothering_native_flora_in_Biodiversity_Rich_Forest.jpg/330px-Parthenium_smothering_native_flora_in_Biodiversity_Rich_Forest.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/69/Parthenium_smothering_native_flora_in_Biodiversity_Rich_Forest.jpg/440px-Parthenium_smothering_native_flora_in_Biodiversity_Rich_Forest.jpg 2x" width="220"/>
       </a>
       <div class="thumbcaption">
        <div class="magnify">
         <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Parthenium_smothering_native_flora_in_Biodiversity_Rich_Forest.jpg" title="Enlarge">
         </a>
        </div>
        Alien invasive species
        <i>
         Parthenium hysterophorus
        </i>
        smothering native flora in Achanakmar Tiger Reserve, Bilaspur, Chhattisgarh, India.
       </div>
      </div>
     </div>
     <h4>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="Genetic_pollution">
       Genetic pollution
      </span>
      <span class="mw-editsection">
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        [
       </span>
       <a href="/w/index.php?title=Invasive_species&amp;action=edit&amp;section=23" title="Edit section: Genetic pollution">
        edit
       </a>
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        ]
       </span>
      </span>
     </h4>
     <div class="hatnote" role="note">
      Main article:
      <a href="/wiki/Genetic_pollution" title="Genetic pollution">
       Genetic pollution
      </a>
     </div>
     <p>
      Native
      <a href="/wiki/Species" title="Species">
       species
      </a>
      can be threatened with
      <a href="/wiki/Extinction" title="Extinction">
       extinction
      </a>
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-76">
       <a href="#cite_note-76">
        [76]
       </a>
      </sup>
      through the process of
      <i>
       <a href="/wiki/Genetic_pollution" title="Genetic pollution">
        genetic pollution
       </a>
      </i>
      . Genetic pollution is unintentional
      <a href="/wiki/Hybrid_(biology)" title="Hybrid (biology)">
       hybridization
      </a>
      and
      <a href="/wiki/Introgression" title="Introgression">
       introgression
      </a>
      , which leads to homogenization or replacement of local
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Genotypes" title="Genotypes">
       genotypes
      </a>
      as a result of either a numerical or
      <a href="/wiki/Fitness_(biology)" title="Fitness (biology)">
       fitness
      </a>
      advantage of the introduced species.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-77">
       <a href="#cite_note-77">
        [77]
       </a>
      </sup>
      Genetic pollution can operate either through introduction or through habitat modification, bringing previously isolated species into contact. Hybrids resulting from rare species that interbreed with abundant species can swamp the rarer species' gene pool. This is not always apparent from
      <a href="/wiki/Morphology_(biology)" title="Morphology (biology)">
       morphological
      </a>
      observations alone. Some degree of
      <a href="/wiki/Gene_flow" title="Gene flow">
       gene flow
      </a>
      is normal, and preserves constellations of
      <a href="/wiki/Gene" title="Gene">
       genes
      </a>
      and genotypes.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-78">
       <a href="#cite_note-78">
        [78]
       </a>
      </sup>
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-79">
       <a href="#cite_note-79">
        [79]
       </a>
      </sup>
      An example of this is the interbreeding of migrating
      <a href="/wiki/Coyote" title="Coyote">
       coyotes
      </a>
      with the
      <a href="/wiki/Red_wolf" title="Red wolf">
       red wolf
      </a>
      , in areas of eastern North Carolina where the red wolf was reintroduced.
      <sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">
       [
       <i>
        <a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed">
         <span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (December 2011)">
          citation needed
         </span>
        </a>
       </i>
       ]
      </sup>
     </p>
     <h2>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="Study">
       Study
      </span>
      <span class="mw-editsection">
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        [
       </span>
       <a href="/w/index.php?title=Invasive_species&amp;action=edit&amp;section=24" title="Edit section: Study">
        edit
       </a>
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        ]
       </span>
      </span>
     </h2>
     <div class="hatnote" role="note">
      See also:
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Invasion_biology_terminology" title="Invasion biology terminology">
       Invasion biology terminology
      </a>
     </div>
     <table class="wikitable" style="float:right;">
      <tr>
       <th>
        Stage
       </th>
       <th>
        Characteristic
       </th>
      </tr>
      <tr>
       <td>
        0
       </td>
       <td>
        Propagules residing in a donor region
       </td>
      </tr>
      <tr>
       <td>
        I
       </td>
       <td>
        Traveling
       </td>
      </tr>
      <tr>
       <td>
        II
       </td>
       <td>
        Introduced
       </td>
      </tr>
      <tr>
       <td>
        III
       </td>
       <td>
        Localized and numerically rare
       </td>
      </tr>
      <tr>
       <td>
        IVa
       </td>
       <td>
        Widespread but rare
       </td>
      </tr>
      <tr>
       <td>
        IVb
       </td>
       <td>
        Localized but dominant
       </td>
      </tr>
      <tr>
       <td>
        V
       </td>
       <td>
        Widespread and dominant
       </td>
      </tr>
     </table>
     <p>
      While the study of invasive species can be done within many subfields of biology, the majority of research on invasive organisms has been within the field of
      <a href="/wiki/Ecology" title="Ecology">
       ecology
      </a>
      and
      <a href="/wiki/Geography" title="Geography">
       geography
      </a>
      where the issue of biological invasions is especially important. Much of the study of invasive species has been influenced by
      <a href="/wiki/Charles_Sutherland_Elton" title="Charles Sutherland Elton">
       Charles Elton's
      </a>
      1958 book
      <i>
       The Ecology of Invasion by Animals and Plants
      </i>
      which drew upon the limited amount of research done within disparate fields to create a generalized picture of biological invasions.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-IE_80-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-IE-80">
        [80]
       </a>
      </sup>
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Lowry_81-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-Lowry-81">
        [81]
       </a>
      </sup>
      Studies on invasive species remained sparse until the 1990s when research in the field experienced a large amount of growth which continues to this day.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Lowry_81-1">
       <a href="#cite_note-Lowry-81">
        [81]
       </a>
      </sup>
      This research, which has largely consisted of field observational studies, has disproportionately been concerned with
      <a href="/wiki/Terrestrial_plant" title="Terrestrial plant">
       terrestrial plants
      </a>
      .
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Lowry_81-2">
       <a href="#cite_note-Lowry-81">
        [81]
       </a>
      </sup>
      The rapid growth of the field has driven a need to standardize the language used to describe invasive species and events. Despite this, little standard terminology exists within the study of invasive species which itself lacks any official designation but is commonly referred to as "Invasion ecology" or more generally "Invasion biology".
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-IE_80-1">
       <a href="#cite_note-IE-80">
        [80]
       </a>
      </sup>
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Lowry_81-3">
       <a href="#cite_note-Lowry-81">
        [81]
       </a>
      </sup>
      This lack of standard terminology is a significant problem, and has largely arisen due to the interdisciplinary nature of the field which borrows terms from numerous disciplines such as agriculture, zoology, and pathology, as well as due to studies on invasive species being commonly performed in isolation of one another.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-IE_80-2">
       <a href="#cite_note-IE-80">
        [80]
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      In an attempt to avoid the ambiguous, subjective, and pejorative vocabulary that so often accompanies discussion of invasive species even in scientific papers, Colautti and MacIsaac proposed a new nomenclature system based on
      <a href="/wiki/Biogeography" title="Biogeography">
       biogeography
      </a>
      rather than on
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Taxa" title="Taxa">
       taxa
      </a>
      .
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-autogenerated3_82-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-autogenerated3-82">
        [82]
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      By discarding taxonomy, human health, and economic factors, this model focused only on ecological factors. The model evaluated individual populations rather than entire species. It classified each population based on its success in that environment. This model applied equally to indigenous and to introduced species, and did not automatically categorize successful introductions as harmful.
     </p>
     <div style="clear:both;">
     </div>

        <span class="reference-text">
         <cite class="citation journal">
          Colautti, Robert I.; Hugh J. MacIsaac (2004).
          <a class="external text" href="http://planet.botany.uwc.ac.za/nisl/Invasives/Assignment1/ColauttiandMacIsaac.pdf" rel="nofollow">
